February 02, 2026 
SUBJECT:
Humboldt Bike Lanes Alternative — Update
RECOMMENDATION:
Receive a presentation from staff on efforts to explore potential alternatives to a portion of the Humboldt Street bike 
lanes and receive direction/recommendation from City Council. 
BACKGROUND:
In 2020, the City Council adopted the Bicycle Master Plan (BMP), which identifies projects and programs organized 
around five goals: Connectivity, Safety and Comfort, Community, Equity, and Ridership. All BMP projects were 
subsequently prioritized, resulting in more than 100 planned improvements. The Humboldt Street bike lane segment 
ranked among the top three priorities citywide. The following timeline summarizes how the Humboldt Street bike lanes 
were constructed, community response to the project, and how it led to additional outreach looking into the feasibility 
of an alternative route:

July 2020 – The program year for the FY 2020-21 federal Community Development Block Grant (CDBG) funding 
from the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD). A portion of these funds was allocated 
towards street and sidewalk improvements, with a goal of repairing deteriorated streets, as well as providing 
accessibility and public safety improvements to streets and sidewalks in the CDBG Priority Neighborhood. 
Specifically, City Council tasked Public Works with prioritizing implementation of its bike master plan, as noted in 
the 2020-21 Annual Action Plan (AAP) to HUD. The project, which included improvements to target this goal and to 
help implement the bike master plan, was titled the North Central Bike Lanes Project (Project).

May 2021 – Adoption of the 2021-22 AAP to HUD, which discussed planned use of funding for pedestrian and 
bicycle improvements for the Project, showing continued progress towards meeting the goal of street and 
sidewalk improvements in a CDBG priority neighborhood. 

July 2021 – Design completed for the North Central Bike Lanes Project.

November 2021 – Construction began on the paving and pedestrian improvements portion of the North 
Central Bike Lanes Project.

February 2022 – City Council authorized installation of the bike lanes on Humboldt Street as part of the North 
Central Bike Lanes Project. The Project included, but was not limited to, approximately 1.3 miles of Class II 
bicycle lanes on Humboldt Street between 5th Avenue and Peninsula Avenue and required the removal of 
approximately 170 parking spaces on Humboldt Street.

July 2022 – Construction of the North Central Bike Lanes Project was completed.

August 2022 – March 2023 – Residents reported that the parking loss associated with the new bike lanes was 
having a significant negative impact on the North Central neighborhood, especially those residents living 
directly on Humboldt Street.

May 2023 – City Council appropriated funds for a new initiative, the North Central Complete Streets 
Plan, to address circulation and parking impacts resulting from the North Central Bike Lanes Project.
58 of 368

--- page 59 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO

June 2023 – May 2024 – Through outreach for the North Central Complete Streets Plan and other 
community contacts, staff continued to receive feedback highlighting ongoing parking loss impacts caused 
by the 2022 bike lane installation on Humboldt Street.
 
At the June 17, 2024, City Council meeting, residents reiterated ongoing concerns about difficulty finding parking in 
the North Central neighborhood that was exacerbated by the Project. City Council directed staff to begin 
investigating additional ways to address community concerns. In response, staff hosted two workshops: In October 
2024, staff met with the smaller North Central Lived Experience Group, which was formed as part of the North 
Central Complete Streets Plan (NCCSP), to discuss the bike lanes on Humboldt Street and explore potential 
alternatives that might help alleviate impacts. In December 2024, staff held a larger community workshop at the 
Martin Luther King Jr. Center, where more than 110 residents attended in person to provide feedback on the bike 
lanes on Humboldt Street, post-installation.
PHASE I DIRECTION FROM CITY COUNCIL
On February 3, 2025, City Council held a Study Session where staff presented the feedback from the October and 
December 2024 meetings as well as the NCCSP and sought Council direction on how to address community impacts 
resulting from the Project. Staff presented the five (5) following (paraphrased) alternatives for Council 
consideration:

Alternative #1 - Keep As-Is: Retains the Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street and do not restore any parking.
 

Alternative #2 - Bicycle Boulevard on Humboldt Street: Removal of the existing Class II bike lanes on 
Humboldt Street, replacement with a bike boulevard with level 2 and 3 traffic calming devices, and 
restoration of approximately 170 parking spaces on Humboldt Street from Peninsula Avenue to 5th 
Avenue.
 

Alternative #3 - Partial Removal of Bike Lanes, and Establishment of a Bike Boulevard on a Parallel 
Street: This alternative was broken down into a short-term plan and a long-term plan.

Humboldt Street Parking Restoration (2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue Only): In the short-term 
restore approximately 100 parking spaces on Humboldt Street from 2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue 
and establish a north-south street parallel to Humboldt Street as a pilot bike boulevard between 2nd 
Avenue and Indian Avenue.

Pilot Parallel Bike Boulevard: Implementation of a pilot bike boulevard on a different street (Idaho 
Street or Fremont Street) after community vetting.

Long-Term Plan: In the long-term perform a connectivity study in the North Central 
neighborhood to reanalyze destinations within the community and see how to improve bike 
connectivity north of Indian Avenue and south of 2nd Avenue and further within the North 
Central neighborhood.

Alternative #4 - Restore Parking with No Bike Infrastructure: Remove bike lanes and restore 
approximately 170 parking spaces on Humboldt Street from Peninsula Avenue to 5th Avenue.

Alternative #5 - Streetlighting Upgrades: Upgrade existing cobrahead streetlight fixtures to brighter 
fixtures to help address community concerns about low lighting while assessing negative effects of 
additional light on residential properties.
 
City Council directed staff to further explore the feasibility and community support surrounding Alternatives 3 and 
5. In particular, the City Council was interested in piloting the streetlighting upgrades associated with Alternative 5, 
and they were interested in engaging the community further, especially the residents who live on Fremont and 
Idaho Street, to understand whether the community was in support of Alternative 3.
 
59 of 368

--- page 60 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
PHASE II ENGAGEMENT
Phase II evaluated the feasibility of Alternatives 3 and 5 through an extensive and inclusive community engagement 
process. The strategy centered on three public workshops and one pilot streetlighting demonstration, all designed 
to gather direct public input. To ensure broad participation, staff implemented a multi-faceted outreach campaign 
that included:
 

Partnering with trusted local networks to amplify awareness

Mailing over 35,000 notices to residents and stakeholders

Distributing more than 1,000 flyers along the corridor and at City facilities

Conducting four citywide surveys

Placing over 140 A-frame signs throughout the North Central community

Performing targeted door-to-door canvassing 

Sustained digital promotion via Nextdoor, Instagram, Facebook, X, the City’s website, and community 
partner networks

Meetings with administrators and faculty from Fiesta Gardens International School and San Mateo High School
 
This comprehensive effort generated over 50,000 digital and in-person engagements (likes, shares, comments, 
views, and QR code scans), successfully driving community participation at in-person and virtual workshops and 
providing critical feedback. Recognizing the linguistic and cultural diversity of the North Central community, all 
engagement activities prioritized accessibility and inclusion:
 

Workshops were held in person at accessible, familiar venues, including the Martin Luther King Jr. 
Center and Fiesta Gardens Elementary School

Evening sessions included childcare, food, and refreshments

Virtual attendance options were optimized for mobile devices and multi-tasking participants

All materials and presentations were provided in English, Spanish, and Chinese

Live interpretation in Spanish and Chinese was offered at all in-person and virtual workshops

Surveys were available in English, Spanish, and Chinese, both online and in print
 
This inclusive, multilingual, and multi-channel approach ensured meaningful participation from a broad cross-
section of the community throughout Phase II. Below is a summary of each of the three workshops and the 
streetlighting pilot efforts. Copies of the presentation slides and concept designs presented at these workshops are 
also available on the City’s website: https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/4871/Community-Workshop-Humboldt-
Street-Bike-. 
 

Workshop No. 1 - Initial Feedback
The first community workshop focused on gathering input on current travel patterns and experiences along 
Humboldt Street, Idaho Street, and Fremont Street. A total of 56 participants signed in at the registration 
table, with several additional attendees who did not sign in but completed surveys. The session began with 
a staff presentation outlining the project goals and City Council direction, followed by facilitated small-
group discussions that followed a structured set of questions to understand the impacts of the existing 
Humboldt Street bike lanes and current usage of the three corridors. Discussion groups reported key 
findings back to identify common themes. 
Summary of Meeting: Overall, the first community workshop was energetic and well-attended, with robust 
participation throughout the evening with approximately 60 attendees. A total of 58 in-person surveys were 
completed, and 155 post-meeting online surveys were received, resulting in 213 responses overall. 
The survey included questions to help staff understand existing uses of Humboldt Street, Idaho Street, and 
Fremont Street, such as for commuting, recreation, or school travel, and by what mode (bike, walking, or 
vehicle). The results of this survey were presented back to the community at the second community meeting 
60 of 368

--- page 61 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
on August 25, 2025 (slides 12-14). A copy of the presentation slides is available on the City’s website: 
https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/4871/Community-Workshop-Humboldt-Street-Bike-.
Participants repeatedly emphasized that the removal of on-street parking to accommodate the existing 
Humboldt Street bike lanes has created a significant burden on residents and surrounding neighborhoods. 
Streetlighting improvements along the Humboldt corridor were very well received, with widespread 
agreement that upgraded lighting would substantially enhance safety and visibility for all users. There was 
no clear consensus on whether Idaho Street or Fremont Street would be the preferred location for a bike 
boulevard. Strong concerns were raised about implementing a bike boulevard on either street due to their 
narrow roadway widths, existing vehicle speeds, traffic congestion, and limited visibility. Several attendees 
proposed Amphlett Boulevard as a viable alternative route that should be considered for bicycle facilities. 
Overall, the feedback highlighted a strong community desire to address parking impacts, improve safety 
through better lighting, and carefully evaluate any new bicycle route to avoid shifting problems to already 
constrained local streets.
 
Workshop No. 2 - Draft Concept Designs
Building on the feedback from Workshop No. 1, the second workshop presented draft concept designs that 
addressed the community’s priorities of not removing more parking beyond what is required under State 
daylighting laws, meaningful speed reduction, and enhanced safety for all users. The draft concepts featured 
two parallel bicycle boulevard alternatives to the existing Humboldt Street facility, both running from Indian 
Avenue to 2nd Avenue: Idaho Street Bike Boulevard and Fremont Street Bike Boulevard. Each option 
incorporated traffic-calming measures designed to lower vehicle speeds and improved safety while 
preserving on-street parking. Participants engaged in group discussions guided by a knowledgeable 
facilitator and used visual aids. Dedicated scribes captured detailed feedback. The structured format allowed 
residents to examine the proposed treatments in detail, ask technical questions, and provide targeted input 
on each corridor, setting the stage for final refinement of the preferred alternative.
 
Summary of Meeting: The second workshop, attended by 38 in-person residents and 9 virtual participants 
with more than 70 survey respondents, centered on the proposed traffic-calming measures for both the 
Idaho Street and Fremont Street bike boulevard alternatives. Attendees showed strong interest in 
examining the specific treatments presented for each corridor. Staff noted specific participant preferences 
for these treatments, based upon feedback received at the meeting and through the survey following the 
meeting, for incorporation into the bike boulevard concept designs:

For the Idaho Street alternative, participants immediately and consistently expressed a clear 
preference for speed humps over rumble strips.

The six proposed intersection safety treatments—developed in direct response to Workshop 
No. 1 feedback emphasizing intersection safety on Idaho Street—were very well received.

High-visibility crosswalks and the introduction of four-way stops at key locations were also 
strongly supported for both alternatives.

Feedback on curb extensions was mixed; several residents noted that they can make turning 
movements more difficult for larger vehicles, while traffic circles were viewed favorably as an 
alternative calming tool.
 
Overall, the proposed calming measures for both alternatives received positive feedback, with participants 
acknowledging that the designs are projected to reduce operating speeds from the current 25 mph to 
approximately 15–18 mph. However, skepticism remained about whether those speed reductions would be 
fully achieved once constructed. Concerns about driveway visibility when vehicles back out were raised 
during both the question-and-answer period and table discussions. There was notable support for creating a 
bike boulevard on either Idaho Street or Fremont Street, with many citing the anticipated reduction in 
vehicle speeds and volumes as a significant benefit. At the same time, there was equally strong sentiment 
against removing the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street, with many participants stating they would not 
support any alternative that eliminates the current bicycle facility.
61 of 368

--- page 62 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
 
Workshop No. 3 - Final Concept Design
This third and final workshop incorporated all feedback received from Workshop No. 2 and presented 
refined final concept designs for both the Idaho Street and Fremont Street bike boulevard alternatives. The 
primary objective was to obtain clear feedback from the community on their preferred outcome:
 

Implementing the Idaho Street bike boulevard;

Implementing the Fremont Street bike boulevard; or

Retaining the existing Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street in their current configuration
 
Staff clarified to participants in attendance as well as in the survey that the bike boulevard alternatives 
proposed for Idaho Street and Fremont Street did not involve removal of parking on these streets (beyond 
what may be required by State daylighting laws), as this was a point of confusion for some community 
members.
 
The session included a concise, high-level staff presentation that highlighted the most significant changes 
made to the bike boulevard concept designs in response to Workshop No. 2 feedback, summarized results 
from the streetlighting pilot demonstration, and strongly encouraged participants to complete a focused 
four-question survey designed to capture the community’s final preferences for the future of bicycle 
facilities on Humboldt Street. The streamlined format and clear choice presented in the survey allowed staff 
to gather decisive input to bring to the Commission and City Council. As this was the final survey of Phase II, 
staff significantly expanded distribution beyond the in-person, virtual, and online options by mailing 
approximately 4,500 prepaid-postage survey mailers to every household and business in the North Central 
community.
 
Summary of Meeting: The third and final workshop was attended by 41 residents in person and 10 virtually. 
Driveway visibility emerged as a recurring concern, with several participants noting increased bicycle usage 
on the narrower Idaho and Fremont corridors could exacerbate sight-line issues for vehicles backing out. 
While participants expressed appreciation for the substantial speed-reduction and safety enhancements 
incorporated into both final alternative designs, a notable portion of attendees maintained that either 
option would still represent a downgrade from the existing protected Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street 
and reiterated their preference to keep the current facility unchanged.
 
Summary of Survey Results:
As a follow up to this meeting, more than 400+ individuals submitted surveys (either in-person, online, or 
mailed). In the survey, staff presented three clear options (as listed below) and asked participants to rank 
them from most preferred to least preferred (1 = most preferred and 3 = least preferred):
 

Keep Existing on Humboldt Street - Retain the existing Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street 
in their current configuration; or

Idaho Street Alternative - Remove the Humboldt Street bike lanes, restore approximately 100 on-
street parking spaces from Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue, and implement the refined Idaho 
Street bike boulevard; or

Fremont Street Alternative - Remove the Humboldt Street bike lanes, restore the same parking 
segment, and implement the refined Fremont Street bike boulevard.
 
Below are the results for the final survey as part of this effort broken into each distribution format (in-
person, online, or mailed). Because it was possible for someone to take the survey via multiple formats (in-
person, online, and mailed), these results are presented separately, and summarized by format, rather than 
presented collectively. All results within each format category have been reviewed and any duplicates or 
attempts to take the online survey multiple times have been removed from the summary below, but the 
results of the online survey results including the duplicates are also included in Attachment 3 (see Tables 3A


In-Person Survey Results
Staff received a total of 40 surveys at the workshop with 34 from residents of the North Central 
neighborhood. When asked directly which of the three alternatives they support, 35% of North 
Central respondents oppose keeping the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street, with 65% in 
support of retaining them. Among residents who live directly on Humboldt Street, the split is more 
drastic: 0% oppose keeping and 100% support keeping the bike lanes, noting that two in-person 
respondents lived on Humboldt Street. The priority ranking indicates a preference across all groups 
to keep the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street compared to the other alternatives. This 
demonstrates consistent neighborhood-wide sentiment to maintain the current Humboldt Street 
configuration.
 

Online Results
Staff received a total of 219 online surveys, with 99 survey responses from residents of the North 
Central neighborhood. When asked directly which of the three alternatives they support, 27% of 
North Central respondents oppose keeping the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street, with 73% 
supporting retaining them. Among residents who live directly on Humboldt Street, the split is nearly 
identical: 26% oppose and 74% support keeping the bike lanes. The priority ranking indicates a 
preference across all groups to keep the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street compared to the 
other alternatives. This demonstrates consistent neighborhood-wide online sentiment for 
maintaining the current Humboldt Street configuration. These results are consistent with the in-
person survey results. 
Staff also reviewed the survey findings inclusive of duplicate IP addresses (Tables 3A and 4A of 
Attachment 3) and determined that the findings did not significantly change, where the majority of 
responses were in support of keeping the bike lanes. 

Mailed Results
Staff distributed prepaid postage mailed surveys to the North Central neighborhood and received a 
total of 150 survey responses. Of these, 42 (28%) were located on Humboldt Street, 13 (9%) were 
located on Idaho Street, 19 (13%) were located on Fremont Street, and 76 (51%) were located in the 
greater North Central area. Other communities outside of North Central were not sent a mailed 
survey.
 
When asked directly which of the three alternatives they support, 68% of North Central 
respondents oppose keeping the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street, with only 32% in support 
of retaining them. Among residents who live directly on Humboldt Street, the split is similar: 66% 
oppose and 34% support keeping the bike lanes. Idaho Street residents in particular were highly 
opposed to a bike boulevard on Idaho Street. This demonstrates consistent neighborhood-wide 
mailed survey sentiment against maintaining the current Humboldt Street configuration. These 
results diverged noticeably from the in-person and online surveys.
Priority Ranking Summary: When respondents were instead asked to rank the three alternatives 
from most to least preferred (1 = most preferred, 3 = least preferred), the results varied between 
North Central and Humboldt Street residents.


Streetlight Pilot
The City conducted a streetlighting pilot between 9/17/25 and 10/15/25. The streetlighting pilot was 
designed to gather direct community feedback on appropriate lighting levels and fixture styles for 
Humboldt Street and for a potential bike boulevard alternative. Two demonstration locations were 
established:
 
o
Humboldt Street: Three cobrahead fixtures were installed side-by-side for comparison — the existing 
standard cobrahead, a medium-intensity version, and a high-intensity version.
o
Idaho Street: Two pedestrian-scale fixtures were displayed — the existing pedestrian-scale standard 
and a high-intensity version. (A medium-intensity pedestrian-scale option was not included because 
the City does not currently have an approved standard for that output level.)
 
Residents were able to view the fixtures at night and provide input on preferred brightness, glare, color 
temperature, and overall suitability for each corridor. Feedback from this pilot was summarized and 
presented at Workshop No. 3 to help inform final lighting recommendations.
 
Survey Summary: Staff received a total of 71 survey responses. Of these, 63% preferred the high-brightness 
option for pedestrian-scale lights, while 46% preferred the high-brightness option for roadway streetlight 
fixtures. Full detailed survey results are provided in Attachment 3 (Table 7 and 8)

School Engagement
On January 23, 2025, the San Mateo-Foster City School District Board unanimously adopted a resolution 
strongly supporting retention of the existing Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street. The resolution describes 
the Humboldt corridor as one of the most vital bicycle segments in the City of San Mateo and states that 
removal of the lanes would pose a threat to safety. On February 1, 2025, staff also received a letter from the 
Parent Teacher Association (PTA) of the San Mateo City School District (SMFCSD) stating their position to 
keep the bike lanes on Humboldt.
 
During Phase II outreach, staff met separately with administrators and faculty from San Mateo High School 
and Fiesta Gardens International School. Both schools emphasized that safety must remain the highest 
priority in any final decision. Fiesta Gardens International School representatives acknowledged the parking 
loss caused by the Humboldt bike lanes and noted that the already-constrained on-street parking supply in 
the surrounding neighborhood indirectly affects staff, families, and visitors. They welcomed any additional 
speed-calming measures on Indian Avenue, which experiences heavy vehicle, pedestrian, and bicycle traffic 
before and after school.

Phase II Engagement Summary
Overall, the engagement efforts made for this entire process were comprehensive and consistent. The feedback 
received was mixed, but there are some common themes, as listed below:
 

Support for measures to slow speeds and volumes in neighborhood streets are welcomed, but not at the 
trade-off of removing parking or existing bicycle infrastructure.

Streetlighting upgrades are well supported as the community perceives it as having a safety benefit.

consistent support for retaining the bike lanes on Humboldt Street by in-person and online survey 
respondents. Mailed survey responses indicated residents living on Humboldt and Idaho Streets 
preferred the Fremont Street bike boulevard over retaining the Humboldt Street bike lanes.

Existing Bicycle Usage Data: During Phase II, staff conducted weekday bicycle counts to establish baseline ridership 
and travel patterns on the three study corridors: Humboldt Street, Idaho Street, and Fremont Street. Counts were 
performed at multiple key intersections during the combined AM + PM peak periods (7:00–9:00 AM and 4:00–6:00 
PM).
 
Key findings are as follows, presented as total weekday AM+PM peak hour bicycle trips for each street, across all study 
intersections:

Humboldt Street (Peninsula Avenue to 3rd Avenue, 7 intersections):
o
343 total intersection bicycle trips
o
Directional split: 68% north–south travel, 32% east–west travel (reflecting strong through-
movement demand along the corridor).

Idaho Street (Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue, 4 intersections):
o
124 total intersection bicycle trips during the same combined peak periods.
o
Directional split: 43% north-south on Idaho Street and 57% east-west

Fremont Street (Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue, 4 intersections):
o
226 total intersection bicycle trips during the same combined peak periods.
o
Directional split: 37% north-south travel on Fremont Street, 63% east-west travel
These counts confirm that Humboldt Street currently carries a large number of bicycle volumes. The data helped 
inform the design of traffic-calming treatments, intersection improvements, and diversion assumptions in the final 
alternatives. Detailed intersection-by-intersection counts are included in Attachment 3 (Table 9).
Potential Idaho Street Alternative
The final bicycle facility concept for Idaho Street, as presented to the community, proposes a potential bike 
boulevard alternative along Idaho Street between Indian Avenue and 2nd Avenue. The potential alternative route is 
approximately 4,000 feet long, compared to the existing 3,200-foot bike facility on Humboldt Street over the same 
general alignment. This alternative incorporates traffic calming and safety improvements along the entire corridor. 
Safety enhancements are provided at seven intersections and include two new traffic circles at Monte Diablo 
Avenue and Indian Avenue and high-visibility crosswalks at all seven intersections. Additionally, eight speed humps 
are proposed along Idaho Street, with a total of ten-speed humps throughout the connecting route. Combined, 
these measures are projected to reduce prevailing vehicle speeds from approximately 25 mph to 15–18 mph and 
lower peak-hour traffic volumes from 130–289 vehicles per hour to 31–150 vehicles per hour. The final proposed 
concept can be found in Attachment 1 – Final Idaho Street Alternative.

Cost Estimate: $1.37M: This cost includes restoration of Humboldt Street from Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue 
to before bike lanes were installed, the design work needed to bring the 15% concept designs to 
construction, construction and inspection services and a contingency. It should be noted that safety 
enhancements are proposed at the intersection of Indian Avenue and Humboldt Street as part of a funded 
and planned project (Local Roadway Safety Plan Priority Package 1) and therefore not included in the cost 
65 of 368

--- page 66 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
estimate.
 
Potential Fremont Street Alternative
The final bicycle facility concept for Fremont Street, as presented to the community, proposes a bike boulevard 
along Fremont Street between Indian Avenue and 2nd Avenue. The existing bike facility on Humboldt Street is 
approximately 3,200 feet long; the new proposed route along Fremont Street extends roughly 4,700 feet before 
reconnecting to the existing Class II bike lane on Humboldt Street. This alternative includes safety improvements at 
ten intersections. Proposed treatments include high-visibility crosswalks, all-way stops, centerline hardening, and 
curb extensions at Monte Diablo Avenue. In total, ten speed cushions are proposed, along with high-visibility 
crosswalks at Cypress Avenue, Tilton Avenue, both Monte Diablo Avenue intersections, Santa Inez Avenue, and 
Indian Avenue. Additionally, the proposal converts 2nd Avenue between S. Grant Avenue and Fremont Street to 
westbound-only motor vehicle traffic, allowing contraflow (two-way) bicycle travel on that segment. Combined, 
these measures are projected to reduce existing vehicle speeds on Fremont Street from 23–26 mph to 15–18 mph 
and lower peak-hour traffic volumes from 100–200 vehicles per hour to 50–120 vehicles per hour. The final 
proposed concept can be found in Attachment 2 – Final Fremont Street Alternative.

Cost Estimate: $1.32M:  This cost includes restoration of Humboldt Street from Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue 
to before bike lanes were installed, the design work needed to bring the 15% concept designs to 
construction, construction and inspection services and a contingency. It should be noted that safety 
enhancements are proposed at the intersection of Indian Avenue and Humboldt Street as part of a funded 
and planned project (Local Roadway Safety Plan Priority Package 1) and therefore not included in the cost 
estimate.
 
Streetlighting Upgrade

Humboldt Street – The limits of the streetlighting upgrade on Humboldt Street will be from Peninsula 
Avenue to 5th Avenue. This segment has approximately 44 existing cobrahead roadway streetlights and 20 
pedestrian scale lights. To upgrade all 66 streetlights, it is estimated to cost approximately $94,000.
 

Idaho Street – The limits of the streetlighting upgrade on Idaho Street is from Indian Avenue to 2nd 
Avenue. This segment has approximately 18 cobrahead roadway streetlights and 9 pedestrian scale lights. 
To upgrade all 27 streetlights will cost approximately $40,500.
 

Fremont Street – The limits of the streetlighting upgrade on Fremont Street will be from Indian Avenue to 
2nd Avenue, the same path as the proposed bike alternative. This segment has approximately 25 cobrahead 
roadway streetlights and 9 pedestrian scale lights. To upgrade all 34 lights would cost approximately 
$47,500.
 
OTHER CONSIDERATIONS

Permit Parking Program
Staff have received feedback from the public as early as October 2024 and during the Phase II engagement 
effort that there was interest in creating a permit parking program on and near Humboldt Street to help 
alleviate the parking issue in the community. At the Sustainability and Infrastructure Commission Meeting 
on December 10, 2026, commissioners also expressed an interest in advancing a permit parking program in 
North Central. 
The City’s current Residential Permit Parking Program (RPPP) policy would require amendments to meet 
the needs of North Central. To better clarify potential amendments that would be needed to the City’s 
current RPPP policy, a summary table is included below. 
Current Policy
Potential North Central Policy Amendment
Current policy restricts RPPPs to areas within 
residential neighborhoods where on-street parking is 
impacted by parked cars from non-residents, or 
Allow implementation of a Permit Parking Program or 
RPPP in North Central with consideration that 
66 of 368

--- page 67 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
parking generators such as a school, business, 
commercial district or commercial use. 
Humboldt Street was impacted by a significant loss of 
parking from the North Central Bike Lanes Project. 
Commercial vehicles are not permitted to park in 
RPPP areas.
Some community members in North Central rely on 
on-street parking to park commercial business 
vehicles. A Permit Parking Program in North Central 
could optionally allow parking of both residential and 
commercial vehicles. 
Other considerations when developing a Permit Parking Program are as follows:
o
If advanced, staff would evaluate the appropriate geographic extent of the program. Because the 
program is intended to address impacts associated with the North Central Bike Lanes Project, staff 
would consider including Humboldt Street and nearby adjacent blocks. The program area would be 
refined to balance effectiveness, equity, and administrative feasibility while targeting areas most 
directly affected by the project.
o
For the program to be effective, a per-house limit on issued permits would need to be determined. 
Staff estimate there are on average 2-3+ vehicles per household, exceeding the availability of on-
street parking supply. It is important to note that most residential parking permit programs are 
designed to manage who may park on a street, not to guarantee a space for every permitted 
vehicle. While a permit parking program wouldn’t add parking capacity to North Central, it could be 
designed to offer impacted residents a “leg up” in finding parking amongst available spaces. 
o
An estimated initial capital investment from $10,000 - $40,000+ is necessary to establish signage 
throughout the community (various depending on program area). On-going costs will include 
permit issuance, permit renewal, enforcement, and sign maintenance which is yet to be defined.

Other Humboldt Projects
Outside of the Humboldt Bike Lane Alternative effort, the City currently has two other active safety 
improvement projects on Humboldt Street and identified additional traffic calming measures that could be 
implemented.
 
o
Poplar/Humboldt Traffic Signal Improvement: This project was completed in late September 2025 
and installed protected left turns at the Poplar Avenue and Humboldt Street intersection. This 
project was listed as the top priority project out of 10 in the City’s adopted Local Roadway Safety 
Plan (LRSP).
o
Local Roadway Safety Plan Priority Package 1: This project was awarded approximately $1 million in 
federal funding to implement Priority Projects 1, 2, 4, and 6 from the City’s adopted LRSP. Priority 
Project 4 specifically includes safety improvements at three intersections along Humboldt Street 
(Humboldt Street/Indian Avenue, Humboldt Street/E. Santa Inez Avenue, and Humboldt 
Street/Tilton Avenue). Improvements include, but are not limited to, all-way stop control, 
intersection lighting upgrades, raised crosswalks, curb extensions, and enhanced sight-distance 
improvements. The project will start the design phase in early 2026.
o
Other Humboldt Street Calming Measures: Based on consistent community feedback in support of 
additional traffic calming measures, staff explored whether additional safety enhancements along 
Humboldt Street between Peninsula Avenue and 2nd Avenue could be implemented. Staff identified 
the option of installing two raised crosswalks between Peninsula Avenue and Poplar Avenue, one at 
the existing crossing at the San Mateo Superior Court and the other at the existing crossing at 
College Way. When combined with all other ongoing Humboldt Street projects, these additional 
measures are projected to reduce prevailing vehicle speeds from the current 24–31 mph to 
approximately 22–26 mph. The two proposed raise crosswalks are estimated to cost approximately 
$130,000, for which there is currently not any identified funding.
HOUSING AND URBAN DEVELOPMENT (HUD) COMMUNITY DEVELOPMENT BLOCK GRANT FUNDING
The City of San Mateo receives annual Community Development Block Grant (CDBG) funding from the HUD. To 
qualify for these funds, the City follows a structured review and approval process. Each year, the City prepares and 
submits an AAP to HUD for approval prior to the allocation of funds. Prior to submission to HUD, the AAP draft is 
published for a 30-day public comment period and is presented to the Community Relations Commission for review 
and recommendation, followed by approval by the City Council. 
The original North Central Bike Lanes Project, which included installation of Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street 
from 2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue, had a total construction contract value of approximately $1.5 million. Of this 
amount, an estimated $273,000 in CDBG funds was used specifically for the bike lane installation on Humboldt 
Street from 2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue. 
 
Should the bike lanes be removed, the portion of CDBG funds expended on the bike lanes (approximately $273,000) 
would need to be credited back to the City’s local CDBG account. HUD may require these funds to be returned to 
HUD first before being reprogrammed back to the City’s CDBG account. 
68 of 368

--- page 69 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
 
In other words, the City Council would need to reallocate funds in the amount of $273,000 from another funding 
source to replace the CDBG funds expended on the bike lanes and restore the City’s local CDBG account in 
accordance with HUD requirements. These reallocated funds would then become available for redistribution to 
other eligible projects within the City of San Mateo, in full compliance with HUD program requirements and national 
objectives.
POTENTIAL ALTERNATIVES 
Staff is providing a range of potential alternatives for Council consideration to address community concerns about safety 
and the loss of parking on Humboldt Street, as described below. These alternatives take into consideration the feedback 
from community engagement process, including the consistent and widespread support for additional traffic 
calming/safety measures and enhanced lighting and the more mixed feedback on keeping/removing the Humboldt Street 
bike lanes vs. installing bike boulevards on Idaho or Fremont Streets. Note that three of the four alternatives below include 
streetlighting upgrades, and their associated costs are reflected in the totals shown for each respective alternative. If the 
Council decides to pursue streetlighting upgrades independently from the alternatives below, the estimated cost per 
street is provided in the table below.

This alternative retains the Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street and does not restore any parking. This option 
maintains existing bike infrastructure, which allows for consistent North-South bike travel from 5th Avenue to 
Peninsula Avenue on Humboldt Street. This option does not address the comments from the community about 
the need to increase parking supply.

Alternative #2 – Keep-As-Is with Additional Humboldt Street Calming Measures + Streetlighting 
Upgrades + Explore Permit Parking Program
This alternative retains the existing Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street (no parking restoration) and 
includes the following additional improvements not provided in the LRSP Priority 1 project: streetlighting 
upgrades along the entire Humboldt Street corridor from Peninsula Avenue to 5th Avenue and 
implementation of enhanced traffic-calming measures on Humboldt Street beyond the scope of the LRSP 
Priority Package 1 improvements. More specifically, these improvements include the installation of two 
raised crosswalks between Peninsula Avenue and Poplar Avenue. As part of this alternative, staff will further 
explore the establishment of a permit parking program tailored to the North Central neighborhood.

estimated initial capital costs for a permit parking program.)

Street from Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue. This alternative would also remove a segment of the existing 
Class II bicycle facility on Humboldt Street from Indian Avenue to 2nd Avenue and restore approximately 100 
parking spaces in that same segment. This alternative is estimated to lower vehicle speeds on Idaho Street

services, and contingency. Additionally, $273,000 would need to be appropriated back into the CDBG 
local account for removal of the Humboldt bike lanes, as described above. While this is an additional 
cost, it is funds that would be available for reinvestment within the City’s CDBG areas.)
 
Sustainability and Infrastructure Commission Feedback
On December 10, 2025, City staff presented the Humboldt Street Bike Lane Alternatives Project (Phase II) to the 
Sustainability and Infrastructure Commission (Commission) for feedback and a recommendation to the City Council. The 
Agenda Report was substantially similar to this report (although this report was updated to address questions raised at 
and subsequent to that meeting) and staff presentation provided a comprehensive overview of the Project history and 
the City Council's direction from the February 3, 2025, meeting. The item received a total of 31 public comments during 
the Commission meeting. Comments reflected a range of perspectives, from strong emphasis on maintaining safety 
through retention of the bike lanes on Humboldt Street (21 commenters) to support for removing the bike lanes and 
restoring parking without implementing an alternative bicycle connection. Commissioner feedback was largely consistent 
in favoring retention of the bike lanes on Humboldt Street, while also advocating for enhancements such as improved 
street lighting and measures to provide additional parking relief for the North Central community. Following discussion, 
the Commission voted unanimously (5-0) to recommend Alternative #2: Retain the existing bike lanes on Humboldt Street 
(Keep-As-Is), with the addition of traffic calming measures, streetlighting upgrades, and exploration of a Residential 
Permit Parking Program to address community parking needs. 
Staff received various questions from Council members and the public after the Commission meeting. Staff provided a 
summary of questions and answers in Attachment 4. 
NEXT STEPS:
•
February 2, 2026 (this meeting): City Council to provide a recommendation on which alternative to advance as it 
relates to the Humboldt Street Bike Lanes. 
•
Depending upon the alternative selected, staff would return to City Council to appropriate funding for the

ATTACHMENT 4
QUESTIONS AND ANSWERS REGARDING HUMBOLDT BIKE LANES ALTERNATIVES
POST 12/10/25 SUSTAINABILITY AND INFRASTRUCTURE COMMISSION MEETING
Background
City staff presented the Humboldt Bike Lanes Alternatives to the Sustainability and 
Infrastructure Commission on December 10, 2025, seeking feedback in advance of the 
upcoming City Council meeting on February 2, 2026. 
Since the meeting, several questions have been raised by Council members and members of 
the public. For any questions not directly addressed by information in the Agenda Report, staff 
have consolidated those questions and corresponding responses below to provide clarity and 
ensure consistent information is available to the public and decision-makers.
Questions and Answers 
1.
It was mentioned in the 12/10/2025 commission meeting that City staff removed 
duplicate IP addresses from the Workshop 3 – Survey. What was the justification 
for this?
•
Answer: City staff removed duplicate IP addresses from the Workshop 3 Survey 
results to help ensure the integrity and accuracy of the public input process. 
There have been multiple concerns raised from the public about potential 
attempts by individuals to submit duplicate or fraudulent responses to skew the 
data. Removing duplicates based on IP addresses is a standard practice in 
online surveys to prevent one person or entity from artificially inflating results, 
while still preserving legitimate unique submissions. This approach is consistent 
with the pre-paid mailed survey process, which limits responses to one per 
household. This step was taken transparently to maintain the reliability of the 
survey data presented to the Commission. However, in order to understand what 
the impact the duplicate IP addresses may have had on the findings, staff have 
also included, as Tables 3A and 4A of Attachment 3, the results without these 
surveys removed. The findings were similar before and after the screening of 
duplicate IP addresses. 
2.
What action items from the 12/10/2025 Sustainability and Infrastructure 
Commission meeting are staff following up on? 
•
Answer: Staff are incorporating commission feedback into the agenda report and 
presentation to City Council in February 2026. At the February 2026 meeting, 
staff will be asking City Council for further direction given both community and 
commission feedback.  
3.
Has staff considered converting streets in North Central into one-way streets? 
How feasible would this be? What is the cost? What are the potential downstream 
impacts by converting one street to one way, against the broader area etc.? 
79 of 368

--- page 80 ---
•
Answer: From the City’s outreach on both the North Central Complete Streets 
Plan (NCCSP) as well as our recent outreach effort, conversion of one-way 
streets is an idea of potential interest to the community for East-West direction 
(but not North-South direction) streets. Conversion of streets to one-way is 
feasible but is not a concept that can be studied lightly and not without significant 
cost. It would take a complex traffic and circulation study to evaluate the cost and 
potential downstream impacts to North Central and the surrounding 
neighborhoods. The cost to convert Humboldt to a one-way street could exceed 
$10 million to cover the cost of the traffic and circulation study, new traffic 
signals, revised signage and striping, and community outreach/engagement.  As 
such, staff believes this option is financially infeasible.
4.
There was a comment from one of the commissioners regarding asking elected 
officials at the state level to follow up and see if the DMV parking lot can be used 
for overnight parking. Are we following up with state representatives? 
o
Answer: Though we heard from one of the commissioners that they would like to 
pursue this further with state representatives, we have heard strong feedback 
from the community when vetting this further that the DMV parking lot is too far to 
walk from Humboldt Street and the 6AM exit timeframe is highly restrictive. DMV 
representatives have also been clear and responsive about their requirements for 
overnight parking, without the need to escalate to state representatives. These 
requirements are summarized in the Agenda Report to the SIC and the 
presentation: 
▪
Leases will be on a non-exclusive basis; agreements are negotiated at 
market rate and limit the types of use and hours of use. Staff often arrive 
around 6AM so vehicles will need to be out of the lot before 6AM. 
Insurance as well as daily routine cleanup will be required.
Staff does not believe this meets the needs of the community and that further 
exploration is not warranted.
5.
There is confusion regarding the feasibility of implementing a residential parking 
permit program in the North Central area. Can the City confirm its current official 
policy on residential parking permits, and if a permit parking program can be used 
in North Central? 
•
Answer: City Council can direct staff to return with amendments to the existing 
permit parking program to meet the needs of North Central or create a new 
program. A permit parking program wouldn’t add parking capacity to North 
Central, but it would offer residents a “leg up” in finding parking amongst 
available spaces. Staff anticipate some additional vetting and community 
outreach would be necessary to confirm the extents and elements of the permit 
parking program. Please note that vehicles are not permitted to park in a bike 
lane, and the permit parking program would not affect this. Parking spaces would 
only be allocated from legal parking spaces.

81 of 368

--- page 82 ---
8.
Has the City considered removing the sidewalks on Humboldt Street to create 
more space? Is there any state law that prohibits this? 
•
Answer: Removing sidewalk is financially infeasible. It will involve the demolition 
of the existing sidewalk, planter strip and relocation of the overhead utility poles. 
This option is estimated to cost over $2.5M for construction and that is just for the 
2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue and does not include utility relocation costs. No 
specific state law prohibits sidewalk removal in general; however, such actions 
must comply with applicable California codes (e.g., Americans with Disabilities 
Act requirements for accessible pathways, California Vehicle Code, and local 
complete streets policies). Given the substantial financial implications and the 
City's current budget constraints, staff does not recommend pursuing this 
approach.
9.
Did the project team collect data on existing speeds on Humboldt? Fremont and 
Idaho? 
As part of the Humboldt Bike Lanes Alternative project, the project team collected speed 
data on Humboldt Street, Idaho Street and Fremont Street to determine appropriate 
calming measures to reduce speeds on roadways to make it suitable for a bicycle 
boulevard. The existing speeds for the three roadways from Indian Avenue to 2nd 
Avenue are shown below:
i.
Humboldt: 24 – 30mph 
ii.
Idaho Street: 23-25mph
iii.
Fremont Street: 23-26mph
10. What’s the latest on partial restoration of parking? What would that look like?
•
Answer: The direction provided by Council on February 3, 2025 was to explore, 
through a community vetting process, whether bike boulevards from Indian to 2nd 
Avenue on either Idaho or Fremont could provide equivalent safety and 
connectivity to the existing bike lanes. If implemented, this could restore 
approximately 100 parking spaces. There is no way to restore parking on 
Humboldt without removing the bike lanes on Humboldt Street.
11. Why didn’t we consider Amphlett Blvd for a Bike Lane? Did City Staff ever look at 
this as an option? 
•
Answer: City Council direction from February 3, 2025, specifically focused 
exploration on Idaho Street and Fremont Street as potential bike boulevard 
alternatives. Staff prioritized these streets accordingly during community 
outreach efforts. During the first community workshop in June 2025, participants 
were invited to suggest additional roadways for consideration. While there was 
no broad consensus on alternatives, a few small groups and some survey 
respondents expressed interest in Amphlett Boulevard as a bike boulevard 
82 of 368

--- page 83 ---
option. In response, staff conducted a high-level evaluation of Amphlett Boulevard's 
suitability for a bike boulevard. Key findings include: 
o
The street is highly commercial, with frequent large-vehicle access from 
driveways. 
o
It is farther from Humboldt Street (reducing direct connectivity). 
o
It serves as a designated truck route. 
o
Average speeds from 2nd Avenue to Indian Avenue are approximately 36 
mph, well above the recommended maximum of 20 mph for bike 
boulevards per guidelines from the National Association of City 
Transportation Officials (NACTO) and American Association of State 
Highway and Transportation Officials (AASHTO).
Due to these factors—particularly the elevated vehicle speeds—staff determined 
that Amphlett Boulevard is not a viable route for a bike boulevard. Regarding 
dedicated bike lanes (rather than a bike boulevard), this would require removal of 
a significant number of on-street parking spaces (approximately 90), rendering it 
nonviable as an alternative to the Humboldt Street bike lanes.
12. How much would it cost to build a parking garage at MLK Center? 
•
Answer: A parking garage at MLK Center is not financially feasible given the 
City’s budget constraints. For context, the recently complete Kiku Parking 
Garage, which is an above-ground parking garage in downtown San Mateo, cost 
approximately $25M. Presuming a subsurface garage beneath the playing fields 
at the King Center, costs would likely be even greater.

Dear Members of the San Mateo City Council, 
I am a resident of San Mateo and live in the Parkside neighborhood. I am also a teacher at San Mateo 
High School, and I regularly commute by bicycle using the Humboldt Street bike lanes. I am writing to 
express my strong support for keeping these bike lanes in place. 
As part of this discussion, I reviewed the proposed alternatives and attempted to ride to work using Idaho 
Street instead of Humboldt. That experience made it very clear why the Humboldt bike lanes are so 
important. Idaho Street felt incredibly unsafe: there was not enough space for two cars to pass 
comfortably, and I frequently found myself squeezed between moving vehicles. At one point, I felt forced 
onto the sidewalk simply to protect my own safety. This is not a viable or safe option for cyclists, nor is it 
fair to pedestrians. 
The Humboldt Street bike lanes provide a critical, safer route for people like me who rely on them to 
commute. They make it possible for residents to choose alternatives to driving, which benefits traffic 
flow, public health, and the environment. Removing them would push cyclists onto streets that are not 
designed to safely accommodate both cars and bikes, increasing risk for everyone. 
I understand that parking is a real concern in many parts of San Mateo. However, I do not believe the bike 
lanes are the cause of this problem. Parking shortages are largely the result of zoning changes that 
allowed multi-family housing on single-family lots without requiring sufficient parking. Eliminating bike 
lanes is a short-sighted response to a much larger and more complex issue—one that is tied to housing 
policy and affordability, not bicycle infrastructure. 
I respectfully urge the City Council to keep the Humboldt Street bike lanes and to continue prioritizing 
safe, 
 
Malu Alegria

Subject: Humboldt Street Decision 
 
  
Honorable Mayor and City Council Members, 
 
Please fix the bike lane mistake on Humboldt Street and restore parking now. 
 
There's no shame in fixing something you didn't break. San Mateo's North Central 
neighborhood has been the victim of more than a century of neglect and poor planning 
choices by previous administrations. The neighborhood continues to suffer from the 
legacy of exclusionary land use and zoning policies and the history of segregation in our 
city is reflected in demographic disparities, economic differences, and inadequate 
investment in infrastructure compared to more affluent neighborhoods. The high-density 
population coupled with poor planning has meant that the area had a lack of adequate 
parking even before the bike lane project. For example, we have churches, businesses, 
and multi-family residences that have no or very little dedicated parking. We have two- 
and three-bedroom homes that have a dozen people (or more) living in them. The 
current City Council didn't create these problems, but you have a responsibility to 
mitigate them.  
 
The decision to expend HUD CDBG funding on a project that removed parking for 
hundreds of residents and benefits a very few, mostly affluent people who don't even 
live in the neighborhood was wrong. In principle, most people in the neighborhood are 
not opposed to bike lanes, but this would not have been the highest priority for us -more 
like a "nice to have". The fact that a prior Council voted to impose the parking removal 
on us at the wee hours of the morning, in a vote that was not properly on the meeting 
agenda, and was done over the objection of many residents in the neighborhood was 
bad enough. That the project used funds that would have improved the quality of life 
had they been used for their intended purpose only adds insult to injury. YOU HAVE THE 
OPPORTUNITY To RIGHT THESE WRONGS NOW. 
 
85 of 368

--- page 86 ---
2
In addition to this sad history, the city has frequently relied on incomplete and 
misleading data to support its choices. The counts of bicycle usage of the new bike lanes 
was so sloppy and poorly thought out as to be laughable. Anyone living or traveling 
through the corridor on a regular basis can tell you that the bike lanes are very rarely 
used by anyone. If these bike lanes were being used by residents of the neighborhood 
on a regular basis in a sustained way, we may be having a different conversation. But 
because they serve almost no benefit to local residents, your choice should be easy. 
CHOOSE INVESTMENTS IN INFRASTRUCTURE THAT WILL RECTIFY NEGLECT AND 
DISPARITIES, NOT AMPLIFY THEM. 
 
The fact that some of the loudest proponents of this bike lane project are not residents 
of the North Central neighborhood, or even of our city, should give you pause. The 
Silicon Valley Bicycle Coalition (SVBC) who pushed for this project, for example, are 
mostly not local and not familiar with our local issues or history-but they are skilled at 
manipulating local politics to have their will imposed. They are people with a single 
purpose and often have blinders on about what else is important besides bikable and 
walkable communities which, while a laudable goal, does little to improve the quality of 
life for people who cannot walk or bike to work and are more concerned with day-to-day 
living than "nice to haves". Unbroken sidewalks, adequate street lighting, more and 
safer parks, pedestrian safety, more convenient parking -- all are things we would want 
before a bike lane. But the SVBC (and others) have shown through their disingenuous 
arguments that they don't care about anything besides having more bike lanes. LISTEN 
TO RESIDENTS OF OUR CITY OVER OUTSIDE SPECIAL INTERESTS. 
 
The misleading arguments that have come from SVBC members and others have made 
outrageous and insulting claims. They frame parking as "vehicle storage" that only 
benefits car owners while forgetting that our streets and roads were paid for by gas 
taxes and many residents of our overcrowded neighborhood have few choices. They 
imply that residents of North Central are acting as if they are "entitled" to the parking in 
front of their homes which is a straw man designed to make us look selfish and 
completely misunderstands the living conditions in the community. And finally, 
advocates hint that the majority of residents in the neighborhood support the bike lanes 
which was not true when they were installed and unlikely to be true now. 
 
Bicycling advocates love the safety argument. But safety measures on our streets are 
not "once size fits all" and, in fact, it is known that bike lanes can reduce safety in some 
contexts. Advocates argue that children will be unsafe if the lanes are removed but, in 
the years since the bike lanes were installed, I've never seen a child (and have seen 
very few adults) using the lanes. Anecdotal, but it points to a likelihood that the safety 
gained by the lanes is marginal. Safety improvements should be data-driven, not 
aspirational and we really don't have trustable data either on the safety of the area 
before or after the bike lane installation. Safety can be improved in many ways: speed 
reduction measures, stop sign daylighting, lower speeds with ENFORCEMENT, and 
shared streets (bike boulevards). Cyclist behavior is also a driver of accidents and 
something cyclists rarely talk about and I think we've all seen cyclists so blatantly 
disregarding traffic rules that it seems to be an epidemic. 
 
One of my children was hit by a car while walking in a crosswalk in our neighborhood 
86 of 368

--- page 87 ---
3
when he was a teen. He was only mildly injured but the accident was not caused by lack 
of vicinity due to parking. Poor street lighting, and lax traffic enforcement in the area 
were likely culprits. The point being that, had the money spent on this luxury amenity 
for a few affluent people been properly spent on the kinds of projects the neighborhood 
needs, we would be safer. Cycling advocates ignore this inconvenient fact. Additionally, 
the removal of parking has created secondary safety risks including: 
 
Drivers circle longer looking for parking creating more congestion and distraction. 
 
Residents double-parking, blocking sightlines, or stopping in travel lanes. 
 
Elderly or disabled residents may walk farther, increasing fall or injury risk. 
 
STOP RELYING ON INCOMPLETE/MISLEADING DATA AND SCARE TACTICS.  
 
 
Yes, it will cost some money to right this wrong. But it's high time to rip this Band-Aid

over the last several years might not bother anyone else, residents of North Central 
continue to be severely affected and have their property values impacted on a daily 
basis. As you know, the full cost of the initial project did not come primarily from local 
tax revenue - so arguments stating a "loss" of that full amount are incorrect. Sure, it's a 
bad look to have to re-do something so visible and expensive, but at this point, what are 
we waiting for? The project was poorly planned, poorly implemented, badly 
communicated, not fully thought through, and based on faulty data and assumptions. 
I would love our city to be safer, more walkable, bike-friendly, and comfortable for all.  Dedicated bike 
lanes are certainly a component of such a vision and, in many applications, are important improvements 
to the transportation infrastructure.  This project has not been an example of that because it was ill-
conceived, rushed, and pushed into being by people with an extremist agenda who lack connection to or 
understanding of the impacted community. 
 
RESTORE PARKING NOW. 
  
Thank you for your service to our city.

Strong Opposition to Bike Lane Removal – Prioritize Safety and Sustainability

Dear San Mateo City Council, 
I am writing to express my strong opposition to the council’s decision to remove the Humboldt Street bike lanes in favor 
of restoring parking spaces. This decision is a step backward for the city’s commitment to safety, sustainability, and 
equitable transportation access. 
The bike lanes were installed as part of a federally funded safety initiative in response to serious traffic incidents, 
including a tragic crash that left a cyclist in a coma. Removing them not only disregards the safety of cyclists and 
pedestrians but also undermines efforts to create a more sustainable and accessible city for all residents—especially 
those without cars, who make up a significant percentage of the North Central neighborhood’s population. 
Additionally, the proposed alternative—a "bike boulevard" without designated lanes—fails to provide meaningful 
protection for cyclists. Shared roadways do not offer the same level of safety as dedicated infrastructure, and removing 
bike lanes will discourage active transportation at a time when cities across the country are investing in more bike-
friendly solutions, not fewer. 
Rather than spending taxpayer dollars to dismantle existing, functional infrastructure, I urge the council to prioritize 
safe, multimodal transportation options that serve all residents, not just those with cars. San Mateo has an opportunity 
to lead by example, fostering a city that is forward-thinking and inclusive. Please reconsider this decision and work 
toward solutions that prioritize safety, sustainability, and equity. 
Sincerely, 
Daniel Case

February 11, 2025 10:28 am

This is what people expect when someone says “Bicycle Boulevard” — that is not even close to what kids would be getting.

Every good story has at least two sides. This fictional story has at least twenty. But don’t worry, in this post I’m only diving into the most important ones.
But first, we need to set the stage. There are children in the audience. To remind everyone to play nice we are starting this match with a soft jungle song by The
Tokens — The Lion Sleeps Tonight

Background Story

This is the fictional story of two humble bike lanes on fictional Anystreet in a fictional working-class neighborhood in fictional Anytown, USA. These bike lanes are
currently fairly isolated and unconnected. But they are a starting point for a bigger plan to provide a larger network. This network of bike lanes would then connect
this working-class neighborhood to schools, libraries, grocery stores, restaurants, jobs, train stations, downtown businesses, neighboring towns, and recreational
opportunities. Currently, these bike lanes benefit many AnyStreet residents- directly or indirectly. They are and will benefit anyone who wants to save $12,000 per
year on car ownership and $2 per mile on car driving costs. They are and will benefit current and future students. They are and will benefit current and future bike

Strive San Mateo General Plan 2040 directs City investment to public improvements

that address health and infrastructure disparities in equity priority communities,
including the North Central and North Shoreview neighborhoods. City investments
will also support active and healthy lifestyles, reducing health disparities around
the city, improving access to fresh and health foods, making parks and open space
equitably accessible for all residents, and making streets safer and more beautiful.

What an Equity Focus Area in Anytown, USA really needs. [source: cityofsanmateo. org]

Here come the Judges

The five judges have access to all the data provided by the ‘Friends of Bike Lanes’ and by all these organizations, Policies and Guidelines:

SB375 — “Sustainable Communities Act”, SB743 (2013), Intermodal Surface Transportation Efficiency Act of 1991 (ISTEA), California Clean Air Act (1988),
American Heart Association (AHA), American Lung Association (ALA), American Cancer Society, American Association of People with Disabilities (AAPD),
ADA, BFP, ARB, CARB, BAAQMD, BATFCA, AARP, CARE, FAST, SR2S, SR2T, RBP, RBN, Complete Streets Movement, Livable Communities, Bloomberg

Decent guides on how to build bike lanes: U.S. Department of Transportation (USDOT), American Association of State Highway and Transportation Officials
(AASHTO) , AASHTO Bike Guide (1974), NACTO, The Netherlands, Denmark, Germany, Paris, Sevilla, ... you name it.

Strive San Mateo General Plan 2040 supports a multimodal transportation system

implemented using a complete streets approach that emphasizes safety and access
for walking, bicycling, transit, and driving. By prioritizing mobility options and
connectivity for all modes, the General Plan works toward reducing congestion on
local streets, vehicle miles traveled and greenhouse gas GHG emissions.

Anytown’s General Plan 2040 [source: cityofsanmateo.org]

The five judges also just heard a bunch of high school teenagers — who are already riding bicycles to school — talk about how great it would be if more children could
be riding.

FINALLY - Let the Judges announce the winner of ‘The Rumble in The Jungle”

had to do was promise some improvements to the bike lanes and pretend just a little that they care about cars, too. But blame everything on the guidelines and plans
and tell them that the city council’s hands are bound. The city council is usually very good at this.

Now, no actual vote has been taken tonight, and no real action is planned for afterward; this is just some general information gathering. Everybody seems to agree
that “Bike Boulevards” and “Sharing the Road” are not a valid solution.

Now no actual vote is taken tonight, and no real action is planned for afterward; this is just some general information gathering. Everybody seems to agree that “Bike
Boulevards” and “Sharing the Road” are not a solution. The arguments brought forward by these car-only people raise more questions than they answer. The city
council would love to stall and ask for more studies. It looks like Las Vegas money is moving towards a Parking Demand Study to find the discrepancies between a
few people with too many cars and the municipal code combined with the US Census. There are more cars parked on-street than there really should be.
